Circuses were the largest structure regularly built in the Roman world.[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotterMattingly1999303-364) The Flavian Amphitheatre, better known as the [Colosseum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colosseum "Colosseum"), became the regular arena for blood sports in Rome.[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumphrey19861%E2%80%933-365) Many [Roman amphitheatres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Roman_amphitheatres "List of Roman amphitheatres"), [circuses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circus_(building)#List_of_Roman_circuses "Circus (building)") and [theatres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_theatre_(structure) "Roman theatre (structure)") built in cities outside Italy are visible as ruins today.[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumphrey19861%E2%80%933-365) The local ruling elite were responsible for sponsoring spectacles and arena events, which both enhanced their status and drained their resources.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-fatal-191) The physical arrangement of the amphitheatre represented the order of Roman society: the emperor in his opulent box; senators and equestrians in reserved advantageous seats; women seated at a remove from the action; slaves given the worst places, and everybody else in-between.[[347]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-366) The crowd could call for an outcome by booing or cheering, but the emperor had the final say. Spectacles could quickly become sites of social and political protest, and emperors sometimes had to deploy force to put down crowd unrest, most notoriously at the [Nika riots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nika_riots "Nika riots") in 532.[[348]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-367)

[![Image 52](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bestiarii.jpg/250px-Bestiarii.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bestiarii.jpg)

The [Zliten mosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zliten_mosaic "Zliten mosaic"), from a dining room in present-day Libya, depicts a series of arena scenes: from top, musicians; gladiators; [beast fighters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bestiarii "Bestiarii"); and convicts [condemned to the beasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damnatio_ad_bestias "Damnatio ad bestias")[[349]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-368)

The chariot teams were known by the colours they wore, with the two main teams being [the Blues and the Greens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blues_and_Greens_in_the_Byzantine_Empire "Blues and Greens in the Byzantine Empire"). Fan loyalty was fierce and at times erupted into [sports riots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sports_riots "Sports riots").[[350]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-369) Racing was perilous, but charioteers were among the most celebrated and well-compensated athletes.[[351]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson2010238-370) Circuses were designed to ensure that no team had an unfair advantage and to minimize collisions (_naufragia_),[[352]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-371) which were nonetheless frequent and satisfying to the crowd.[[353]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-372) The races retained a magical aura through their early association with [chthonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chthonic "Chthonic") rituals: circus images were considered protective or lucky, [curse tablets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curse_tablet "Curse tablet") have been found buried at the site of racetracks, and charioteers were often suspected of sorcery.[[354]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-373) Chariot racing continued into the Byzantine period under imperial sponsorship, but the decline of cities in the 6th and 7th centuries led to its eventual demise.[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotterMattingly1999303-364)

The Romans thought gladiator contests had originated with [funeral games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Funeral_games_(antiquity) "Funeral games (antiquity)") and [sacrifices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sacrifice_in_ancient_Roman_religion "Sacrifice in ancient Roman religion"). Some of the earliest [styles of gladiator fighting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Roman_gladiator_types "List of Roman gladiator types") had ethnic designations such as "[Thracian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thraex "Thraex")" or "Gallic".[[355]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-374) The staged combats were considered _munera_, "services, offerings, benefactions", initially distinct from the festival games (_ludi_).[[356]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-375) To mark the opening of the Colosseum, [Titus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titus "Titus") presented [100 days of arena events](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inaugural_games_of_the_Flavian_Amphitheatre "Inaugural games of the Flavian Amphitheatre"), with 3,000 gladiators competing on a single day.[[357]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-376) Roman fascination with gladiators is indicated by how widely they are depicted on mosaics, wall paintings, lamps, and in graffiti.[[358]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards200749-377) Gladiators were trained combatants who might be slaves, convicts, or free volunteers.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards200750-378) Death was not a necessary or even desirable outcome in matches between these highly skilled fighters, whose training was costly and time-consuming.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-379) By contrast, _noxii_ were convicts sentenced to the arena with little or no training, often unarmed, and with no expectation of survival; physical suffering and humiliation were considered appropriate [retributive justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retributive_justice "Retributive justice").[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-fatal-191) These executions were sometimes staged or ritualized as re-enactments of [myths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology"), and amphitheatres were equipped with elaborate [stage machinery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stagecraft "Stagecraft") to create special effects.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-fatal-191)[[361]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-380)

Modern scholars have found the pleasure Romans took in the "theatre of life and death"[[362]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-381) difficult to understand.[[363]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-382)[Pliny the Younger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger "Pliny the Younger") rationalized gladiator spectacles as good for the people, "to inspire them to face honourable wounds and despise death, by exhibiting love of glory and desire for victory".[[364]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-383) Some Romans such as [Seneca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger "Seneca the Younger") were critical of the brutal spectacles, but found virtue in the courage and dignity of the defeated fighter[[365]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards200766%E2%80%9367,_72-384)—an attitude that finds its fullest expression with the [Christians martyred](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_martyr "Christian martyr") in the arena. Tertullian considered deaths in the arena to be nothing more than a dressed-up form of [human sacrifice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_sacrifice "Human sacrifice").[[366]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-385) Even [martyr literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acts_of_the_martyrs "Acts of the martyrs"), however, offers "detailed, indeed luxuriant, descriptions of bodily suffering",[[367]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2007212-386) and became a popular genre at times indistinguishable from fiction.[[368]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-387)

### Recreation

[![Image 53](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Casale_Bikini_modified.jpg/250px-Casale_Bikini_modified.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Casale_Bikini_modified.jpg)

So-called "Bikini Girls" mosaic from the [Villa del Casale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Villa_del_Casale "Villa del Casale"), [Roman Sicily](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Sicily "Roman Sicily"), 4th century

The singular _[ludus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludus\_(ancient\_Rome) "Ludus (ancient Rome)")_, "play, game, sport, training", had a wide range of meanings such as "word play", "theatrical performance", "board game", "primary school", and even "gladiator training school" (as in _[Ludus Magnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludus\_Magnus "Ludus Magnus")_).[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-388) Activities for children and young people in the Empire included [hoop rolling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoop_rolling#Ancient_Rome_and_Byzantium "Hoop rolling") and [knucklebones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knucklebones "Knucklebones") (_astragali_ or "jacks"). Girls had [dolls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doll "Doll") made of wood, [terracotta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terracotta "Terracotta"), and especially [bone and ivory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivory_carving "Ivory carving").[[370]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawson2003128-389) Ball games include [trigon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigon_(game) "Trigon (game)") and [harpastum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harpastum "Harpastum").[[371]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-390) People of all ages played [board games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Board_game "Board game"), including _[latrunculi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludus\_latrunculorum "Ludus latrunculorum")_ ("Raiders") and _[XII scripta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludus\_duodecim\_scriptorum "Ludus duodecim scriptorum")_ ("Twelve Marks").[[372]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-391) A game referred to as _alea_ (dice) or _tabula_ (the board) may have been similar to [backgammon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backgammon "Backgammon").[[373]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-games-392)[Dicing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dice "Dice") as a form of gambling was disapproved of, but was a popular pastime during the festival of the [Saturnalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturnalia "Saturnalia").[[374]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-393)

After adolescence, most physical training for males was of a military nature. The [Campus Martius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Campus_Martius "Campus Martius") originally was an exercise field where young men learned horsemanship and warfare. Hunting was also considered an appropriate pastime. According to [Plutarch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plutarch "Plutarch"), conservative Romans disapproved of Greek-style athletics that promoted a fine body for its own sake, and condemned [Nero's efforts to encourage Greek-style athletic games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quinquennial_Neronia "Quinquennial Neronia").[[375]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-394) Some women trained as gymnasts and dancers, and a rare few as [female gladiators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gladiatrix "Gladiatrix"). The "Bikini Girls" mosaic shows young women engaging in routines comparable to [rhythmic gymnastics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhythmic_gymnastics "Rhythmic gymnastics").[[t]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-396)[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-397) Women were encouraged to maintain health through activities such as playing ball, swimming, walking, or reading aloud (as a breathing exercise).[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-398)

### Clothing

[![Image 54](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Statua_togata%2C_dalla_palestra_di_foruli_%28civitatomassa%29%2C_et%C3%A0_claudia.jpg/250px-Statua_togata%2C_dalla_palestra_di_foruli_%28civitatomassa%29%2C_et%C3%A0_claudia.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Statua_togata,_dalla_palestra_di_foruli_(civitatomassa),_et%C3%A0_claudia.jpg)

Togate statue in the [Museo Archeologico Nazionale d'Abruzzo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_d%27Abruzzo "Museo Archeologico Nazionale d'Abruzzo")

In a status-conscious society like that of the Romans, clothing and personal adornment indicated the etiquette of interacting with the wearer.[[379]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010230-399) Wearing the correct clothing reflected a society in good order.[[380]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-coon-400) There is little direct evidence of how Romans dressed in daily life, since portraiture may show the subject in clothing with symbolic value, and surviving textiles are rare.[[381]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-bieber-401)[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-402)

The [toga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toga "Toga") was the distinctive national garment of the male citizen, but it was heavy and impractical, worn mainly for conducting political or court business and religious rites.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVout2009216-403)[[381]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-bieber-401) It was a "vast expanse" of semi-circular white wool that could not be put on and draped correctly without assistance.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVout2009216-403) The drapery became more intricate and structured over time.[[384]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM%C3%A9traux2008282%E2%80%93283-404) The _toga praetexta_, with a [purple or purplish-red](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrian_purple "Tyrian purple") stripe representing inviolability, was worn by children who had not come of age, [curule magistrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Executive_magistrates_of_the_Roman_Empire "Executive magistrates of the Roman Empire"), and state priests. Only the emperor could wear an all-purple toga (_toga picta_).[[385]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-405)

Ordinary clothing was dark or colourful. The basic garment for all Romans, regardless of gender or wealth, was the simple sleeved [tunic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunic "Tunic"), with length differing by wearer.[[386]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010231-406) The tunics of poor people and labouring slaves were made from coarse wool in natural, dull shades; finer tunics were made of lightweight wool or linen. A man of the senatorial or equestrian order wore a tunic with two purple stripes (_clavi_) woven vertically: the wider the stripe, the higher the wearer's status.[[386]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010231-406) Other garments could be layered over the tunic. Common male attire also included cloaks, and in some regions, [trousers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braccae "Braccae").[[387]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVout2009218-407) In the 2nd century, emperors and elite men are often portrayed wearing the [pallium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pallium_(Roman_cloak) "Pallium (Roman cloak)"), an originally Greek mantle; women are also portrayed in the pallium. [Tertullian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertullian "Tertullian") considered the pallium an appropriate garment both for Christians, in contrast to the toga, and for educated people.[[380]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-coon-400)[[381]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-bieber-401)[[388]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-408)

Roman clothing styles changed over time.[[389]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010232-409) In the [Dominate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominate "Dominate"), clothing worn by both soldiers and bureaucrats became highly decorated with geometrical patterns, stylized plant motifs, and in more elaborate examples, human or animal figures.[[390]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-410) Courtiers of the later Empire wore elaborate silk robes. The militarization of Roman society, and the waning of urban life, affected fashion: heavy military-style belts were worn by bureaucrats as well as soldiers, and the toga was abandoned,[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-411) replaced by the pallium as a garment embodying social unity.[[392]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVout2009217-412)

Arts
----

[Greek art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_art "Ancient Greek art") had a profound influence on Roman art.[[393]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKousser20084%E2%80%935,_8-413)[Public art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_art "Public art")—including [sculpture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_sculpture "Roman sculpture"), monuments such as [victory columns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Roman_victory_columns "List of Roman victory columns") or [triumphal arches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triumphal_arch "Triumphal arch"), and the iconography on [coins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_currency "Roman currency")—is often analysed for historical or ideological significance.[[394]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-414) In the private sphere, artistic objects were made for [religious dedications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Votum "Votum"), [funerary commemoration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_funerals_and_burial "Roman funerals and burial"), domestic use, and commerce.[[395]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGazda19911%E2%80%933-415) The wealthy advertised their appreciation of culture through artwork and [decorative arts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decorative_arts "Decorative arts") in their homes.[[396]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-416) Despite the value placed on art, even famous artists were of low social status, partly as they worked with their hands.[[397]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010312%E2%80%93313-417)

### Portraiture

[![Image 55](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/250px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_(M._Prado)_01.jpg)

[![Image 56](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Antinous_Mandragone_profil.jpg/250px-Antinous_Mandragone_profil.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Antinous_Mandragone_profil.jpg)

Portraiture, which survives mainly in sculpture, was the most copious form of imperial art. Portraits during the Augustan period utilize [classical proportions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classicism "Classicism"), evolving later into a mixture of realism and idealism.[[398]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-418) Republican portraits were characterized by [verism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Verism "Verism"), but as early as the 2nd century BC, Greek [heroic nudity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heroic_nudity "Heroic nudity") was adopted for conquering generals.[[399]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-419) Imperial portrait sculptures may model a mature head atop a youthful nude or semi-nude body with perfect musculature.[[400]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010451-420) Clothed in the toga or military regalia, the body communicates rank or role, not individual characteristics.[[401]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-421)

Portraiture in painting is represented primarily by the [Fayum mummy portraits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fayum_mummy_portrait "Fayum mummy portrait"), which evoke Egyptian and Roman traditions of commemorating the dead with realistic painting. Marble portrait sculpture were painted, but traces have rarely survived.[[402]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010453-422)

### Sculpture and sarcophagi

[![Image 57](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/10_2023_-_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Roma%2C_Lazio%2C_00186%2C_Italia_-_Sarcofago_Grande_Ludovisi_%28Grande_Ludovisi_sarcophagus%29_-_Arte_Romana_-_Photo_Paolo_Villa_FO232047_ombre_gimp_bis.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:10_2023_-_Palazzo_Altemps,_Roma,_Lazio,_00186,_Italia_-_Sarcofago_Grande_Ludovisi_(Grande_Ludovisi_sarcophagus)_-_Arte_Romana_-_Photo_Paolo_Villa_FO232047_ombre_gimp_bis.jpg)

On the [Ludovisi sarcophagus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludovisi_sarcophagus "Ludovisi sarcophagus")

Examples of Roman sculpture survive abundantly, though often in damaged or fragmentary condition, including freestanding statuary in marble, bronze and [terracotta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Roman_pottery#Terracotta_figurines "Ancient Roman pottery"), and [reliefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relief "Relief") from public buildings and monuments. Niches in amphitheatres were originally filled with statues,[[403]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKousser200813-423)[[404]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-424) as were [formal gardens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_gardens "Roman gardens").[[405]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010274%E2%80%93275-425) Temples housed cult images of deities, often by famed sculptors.[[406]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010242-426)

Elaborately carved marble and limestone [sarcophagi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarcophagus "Sarcophagus") are characteristic of the 2nd to 4th centuries.[[407]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-427) Sarcophagus relief has been called the "richest single source of Roman iconography",[[408]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElsnerHuskinson201114-428) depicting [mythological scenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mythology "Classical mythology")[[409]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElsnerHuskinson201112-429) or Jewish/Christian imagery[[410]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElsnerHuskinson20111,_9-430) as well as the deceased's life.

### Painting

[![Image 58](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Zeffiro-e-clori---pompeii.jpg/250px-Zeffiro-e-clori---pompeii.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zeffiro-e-clori---pompeii.jpg)

_The Wedding of [Zephyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zephyrus "Zephyrus") and [Chloris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloris "Chloris")_ (54–68 AD, [Pompeian Fourth Style](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pompeian_Styles "Pompeian Styles")) within painted architectural panels from the Casa del Naviglio

Initial Roman painting drew from [Etruscan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etruscan_art#Wall-painting "Etruscan art") and [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_art#Painting "Ancient Greek art") models and techniques. Examples of Roman paintings can be found in [palaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ancient_monuments_in_Rome#Palaces "List of ancient monuments in Rome"), [catacombs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ancient_monuments_in_Rome#Cemeteries "List of ancient monuments in Rome") and [villas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_villa "Roman villa"). Much of what is known of Roman painting is from the interior decoration of private homes, particularly as preserved by the [eruption of Vesuvius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eruption_of_Mount_Vesuvius_in_AD_79 "Eruption of Mount Vesuvius in AD 79"). In addition to decorative borders and panels with geometric or vegetative motifs, wall painting depicts scenes from mythology and theatre, landscapes and gardens, [spectacles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#Spectacles), everyday life, and [erotic art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erotic_art_in_Pompeii_and_Herculaneum "Erotic art in Pompeii and Herculaneum").

### Mosaic

[![Image 59](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Neptune_Roman_mosaic_Bardo_Museum_Tunis.jpg/250px-Neptune_Roman_mosaic_Bardo_Museum_Tunis.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Neptune_Roman_mosaic_Bardo_Museum_Tunis.jpg)

_[The Triumph of Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune\_(mythology) "Neptune (mythology)")_ floor mosaic from [Africa Proconsularis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa_Proconsularis "Africa Proconsularis") (present-day Tunisia)[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010463-431)

[Mosaics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosaic "Mosaic") are among the most enduring of Roman [decorative arts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decorative_arts "Decorative arts"), and are found on floors and other architectural features. The most common is the [tessellated mosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opus_tessellatum "Opus tessellatum"), formed from uniform pieces _([tesserae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tessera "Tessera"))_ of materials such as stone and glass.[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010459-432)_[Opus sectile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opus\_sectile "Opus sectile")_ is a related technique in which flat stone, usually coloured marble, is cut precisely into shapes from which geometric or figurative patterns are formed. This more difficult technique became especially popular for luxury surfaces in the 4th century (e.g. the [Basilica of Junius Bassus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basilica_of_Junius_Bassus "Basilica of Junius Bassus")).[[413]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunbabin1999254ff-433)

[Figurative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figurative_art "Figurative art") mosaics share many themes with painting, and in some cases use almost identical [compositions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composition_(visual_arts) "Composition (visual arts)"). Geometric patterns and mythological scenes occur throughout the Empire. In North Africa, a particularly rich source of mosaics, homeowners often chose scenes of life on their estates, hunting, agriculture, and local wildlife.[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010463-431) Plentiful and major examples of Roman mosaics come also from present-day Turkey (particularly the ([Antioch mosaics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antioch_mosaics "Antioch mosaics")[[414]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-434)), Italy, southern France, Spain, and Portugal.

### Decorative arts

[Decorative arts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decorative_arts "Decorative arts") for luxury consumers included fine pottery, silver and bronze vessels and implements, and glassware. Pottery manufacturing was economically important, as were the glass and metalworking industries. Imports stimulated new regional centres of production. Southern Gaul became a leading producer of the finer red-gloss pottery (_[terra sigillata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra\_sigillata "Terra sigillata")_) that was a major trade good in 1st-century Europe.[[415]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin2010202-435)[Glassblowing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glassblowing "Glassblowing") was regarded by the Romans as originating in Syria in the 1st century BC, and by the 3rd century, Egypt and the [Rhineland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhineland "Rhineland") had become noted for fine glass.[[416]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-436)

*   [![Image 60: Silver cup, from the Boscoreale Treasure (early 1st century AD)](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Skyphos_Boscoreale_Louvre_Bj2367.jpg/330px-Skyphos_Boscoreale_Louvre_Bj2367.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Skyphos_Boscoreale_Louvre_Bj2367.jpg "Silver cup, from the Boscoreale Treasure (early 1st century AD)")

Silver [cup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skyphos "Skyphos"), from the [Boscoreale Treasure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boscoreale_Treasure "Boscoreale Treasure") (early 1st century AD) 
*   [![Image 61: Finely decorated Gallo-Roman terra sigillata bowl](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/C%C3%A9ramique_sigill%C3%A9e_Metz_100109_2.jpg/330px-C%C3%A9ramique_sigill%C3%A9e_Metz_100109_2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:C%C3%A9ramique_sigill%C3%A9e_Metz_100109_2.jpg "Finely decorated Gallo-Roman terra sigillata bowl")

Finely decorated Gallo-Roman _[terra sigillata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra\_sigillata "Terra sigillata")_ bowl 
*   [![Image 62: Gold earrings with gemstones, 3rd century](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Boucles_d%27oreilles_3%C3%A8me_si%C3%A8cle_Mus%C3%A9e_de_Laon_030208.jpg/330px-Boucles_d%27oreilles_3%C3%A8me_si%C3%A8cle_Mus%C3%A9e_de_Laon_030208.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Boucles_d%27oreilles_3%C3%A8me_si%C3%A8cle_Mus%C3%A9e_de_Laon_030208.jpg "Gold earrings with gemstones, 3rd century")

Gold earrings with gemstones, 3rd century

*   [![Image 63: Glass cage cup from the Rhineland, 4th century](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Munich_Cup_Diatretum_22102016_1.jpg/250px-Munich_Cup_Diatretum_22102016_1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Munich_Cup_Diatretum_22102016_1.jpg "Glass cage cup from the Rhineland, 4th century")

Glass [cage cup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cage_cup "Cage cup") from the Rhineland, 4th century

### Performing arts

[![Image 64](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Choregos_actors_MAN_Napoli_Inv9986.jpg/250px-Choregos_actors_MAN_Napoli_Inv9986.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Choregos_actors_MAN_Napoli_Inv9986.jpg)

All-male theatrical troupe preparing for a masked performance, on a mosaic from the [House of the Tragic Poet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_the_Tragic_Poet "House of the Tragic Poet")

In Roman tradition, borrowed from the Greeks, literary theatre was performed by all-male troupes that used face masks with exaggerated facial expressions to portray emotion. Female roles were played by men in [drag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(clothing) "Drag (clothing)") (_[travesti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travesti\_(theatre) "Travesti (theatre)")_).[[417]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-437) Roman literary theatre tradition is represented in [Latin literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#Literature) by the tragedies of [Seneca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger "Seneca the Younger"), for example.

More popular than literary theatre was the genre-defying _mimus_ theatre, which featured scripted scenarios with free improvisation, risqué language and sex scenes, action sequences, and political satire, along with dance, juggling, acrobatics, tightrope walking, striptease, and [dancing bears](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dancing_bear "Dancing bear").[[418]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-438) Unlike literary theatre, _mimus_ was played without masks, and encouraged stylistic realism. Female roles were performed by women.[[419]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-439)_Mimus_ was related to _[pantomimus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pantomime#Ancient\_Rome "Pantomime")_, an early form of [story ballet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Story_ballet "Story ballet") that contained no spoken dialogue but rather a sung [libretto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libretto "Libretto"), often mythological, either tragic or comic.[[420]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-440)

[![Image 65](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Scena_di_commedia%2C_musici_ambulanti%2C_da_villa_di_cecerone_a_pompei%2C_9985%2C_03.JPG/250px-Scena_di_commedia%2C_musici_ambulanti%2C_da_villa_di_cecerone_a_pompei%2C_9985%2C_03.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Scena_di_commedia,_musici_ambulanti,_da_villa_di_cecerone_a_pompei,_9985,_03.JPG)

Trio of musicians playing an _[aulos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aulos "Aulos")_, _cymbala_, and _[tympanum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tympanum\_(hand\_drum) "Tympanum (hand drum)")_ (mosaic from [Pompeii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pompeii "Pompeii"))

Although sometimes regarded as foreign, [music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Rome "Music of ancient Rome") and dance existed in Rome from earliest times.[[421]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaerebout2009146-441) Music was customary at funerals, and the _[tibia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aulos "Aulos")_, a woodwind instrument, was played at sacrifices.[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-klar-442) Song _([carmen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carmen\_(verse) "Carmen (verse)"))_ was integral to almost every social occasion. Music was thought to reflect the orderliness of the cosmos.[[423]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHabinek200590ff-443) Various woodwinds and ["brass" instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brass_instrument "Brass instrument") were played, as were [stringed instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stringed_instruments "Stringed instruments") such as the _[cithara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cithara "Cithara")_, and percussion.[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-klar-442) The _[cornu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cornu\_(horn) "Cornu (horn)")_, a long tubular metal wind instrument, was used for military signals and on parade.[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-klar-442) These instruments spread throughout the provinces and are widely depicted in Roman art.[[424]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-444) The hydraulic pipe organ _([hydraulis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydraulis "Hydraulis"))_ was "one of the most significant technical and musical achievements of antiquity", and accompanied gladiator games and events in the amphitheatre.[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-klar-442) Although certain dances were seen at times as non-Roman or unmanly, dancing was embedded in religious rituals of archaic Rome.[[425]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaerebout2009146ff-445) Ecstatic dancing was a feature of the [mystery religions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mystery_religions "Mystery religions"), particularly the cults of [Cybele](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cybele "Cybele")[[426]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaerebout2009154,_157-446) and [Isis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isis "Isis"). In the secular realm, dancing girls from [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria_(Roman_province) "Syria (Roman province)") and [Cadiz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%A1diz "Cádiz") were extremely popular.[[427]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaerebout2009156%E2%80%93157-447)

Like [gladiators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gladiator "Gladiator"), entertainers were legally _[infames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infamia "Infamia")_, technically free but little better than slaves. "Stars", however, could enjoy considerable wealth and celebrity, and mingled socially and often sexually with the elite.[[428]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-448) Performers supported each other by forming guilds, and several memorials for theatre members survive.[[429]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-449) Theatre and dance were often condemned by [Christian polemicists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_polemic "Christian polemic") in the later Empire.[[421]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaerebout2009146-441)[[430]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-450)

Literacy, books, and education
------------------------------

[![Image 66](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Meister_des_Portr%C3%A4ts_des_Paquius_Proculus_001.jpg/250px-Meister_des_Portr%C3%A4ts_des_Paquius_Proculus_001.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Meister_des_Portr%C3%A4ts_des_Paquius_Proculus_001.jpg)

Pride in literacy was displayed through emblems of reading and writing, as in this portrait of [Terentius Neo and his wife](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portrait_of_Terentius_Neo "Portrait of Terentius Neo") (_c._ 20 AD)

Estimates of the average [literacy rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literacy_rate "Literacy rate") range from 5 to over 30%.[[431]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-451)[[432]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-kraus-452)[[433]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201189,_97%E2%80%9398-453) The Roman obsession with documents and inscriptions indicates the value placed on the written word.[[434]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-454)[[435]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-morgan-455)[[u]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-457) Laws and edicts were posted as well as read out. Illiterate Roman subjects could have a government scribe (_[scriba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scriba\_(ancient\_Rome) "Scriba (ancient Rome)")_) read or write their official documents for them.[[432]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-kraus-452)[[437]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndo2000101-458) The military produced extensive written records.[[438]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-459) The [Babylonian Talmud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonian_Talmud "Babylonian Talmud") declared "if all seas were ink, all reeds were pen, all skies parchment, and all men scribes, they would be unable to set down the full scope of the Roman government's concerns".[[439]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndo200086%E2%80%9387-460)

[Numeracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numeracy "Numeracy") was necessary for commerce.[[435]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-morgan-455)[[440]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattern1999197-461) Slaves were numerate and literate in significant numbers; some were highly educated.[[441]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin201019%E2%80%9320-462) Graffiti and low-quality inscriptions with misspellings and [solecisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solecism "Solecism") indicate casual literacy among non-elites.[[442]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-463)[[v]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-464)[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-curchin-101)

The Romans had an extensive [priestly archive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#libri_pontificales "Glossary of ancient Roman religion"), and inscriptions appear throughout the Empire in connection with [votives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Votum "Votum") dedicated by ordinary people, as well as "[magic spells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magic_in_the_Greco-Roman_world "Magic in the Greco-Roman world")" (e.g. the [Greek Magical Papyri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_Magical_Papyri "Greek Magical Papyri")).[[443]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-465)

Books were expensive, since each copy had to be written out on a papyrus roll (_volumen_) by scribes.[[444]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson201017%E2%80%9318-466) The [codex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Codex "Codex")—pages bound to a spine—was still a novelty in the 1st century,[[445]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-467) but by the end of the 3rd century was replacing the _volumen_.[[446]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-468) Commercial book production was established by the late Republic,[[447]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1976253-469) and by the 1st century certain neighbourhoods of Rome and Western provincial cities were known for their bookshops.[[448]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-470) The quality of editing varied wildly,[[449]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-471) and [plagiarism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plagiarism "Plagiarism") or [forgery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literary_forgery "Literary forgery") were common, since there was no [copyright law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copyright_law "Copyright law").[[447]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1976253-469)

[![Image 67](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Table_with_was_and_stylus_Roman_times.jpg/250px-Table_with_was_and_stylus_Roman_times.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Table_with_was_and_stylus_Roman_times.jpg)

Reconstruction of a [wax writing tablet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wax_tablet "Wax tablet")

Collectors amassed personal libraries,[[450]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1976252%E2%80%93264-472) and a fine library was part of the cultivated leisure (_[otium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otium "Otium")_) associated with the villa lifestyle.[[451]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECavalloChartier199967%E2%80%9368-473) Significant collections might attract "in-house" scholars,[[452]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1976257%E2%80%93260-474) and an individual benefactor might endow a community with a library (as [Pliny the Younger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger "Pliny the Younger") did in [Comum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comum "Comum")).[[453]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-475) Imperial libraries were open to users on a limited basis, and represented a [literary canon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literary_canon "Literary canon").[[454]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-476) Books considered subversive might be publicly burned,[[455]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-477) and [Domitian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domitian "Domitian") crucified copyists for reproducing works deemed treasonous.[[456]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-478)

Literary texts were often shared aloud at meals or with reading groups.[[457]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-479) Public readings (_[recitationes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recitationes "Recitationes")_) expanded from the 1st through the 3rd century, giving rise to "consumer literature" for entertainment.[[458]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECavalloChartier199968%E2%80%9369,_78%E2%80%9379-480) Illustrated books, including erotica, were popular, but are poorly represented by extant fragments.[[459]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECavalloChartier199981%E2%80%9382-481)

Literacy began to decline during the [Crisis of the Third Century](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century "Crisis of the Third Century").[[460]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris19893-482) The emperor Julian banned Christians from teaching the classical curriculum,[[461]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin201019-483) but the [Church Fathers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_Fathers "Church Fathers") and other Christians adopted Latin and Greek literature, philosophy and science in biblical interpretation.[[462]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-484) As the Western Roman Empire declined, reading became rarer even for those within the Church hierarchy,[[463]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECavalloChartier199986-485) although it continued in the [Byzantine Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_Empire "Byzantine Empire").[[464]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECavalloChartier199915%E2%80%9316-486)

### Education

[![Image 68](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Roman_school.jpg/500px-Roman_school.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Roman_school.jpg)

A teacher with two students, as a third arrives with his _loculus_, a writing case[[465]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201195-487)

Traditional Roman education was moral and practical. Stories were meant to instil Roman values (_[mores maiorum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mos\_maiorum "Mos maiorum")_). Parents were expected to act as role models, and working parents passed their skills to their children, who might also enter apprenticeships.[[466]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201184%E2%80%9385-488) Young children were attended by a [pedagogue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paedagogus_(occupation) "Paedagogus (occupation)"), usually a Greek slave or former slave,[[467]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011113%E2%80%93116-489) who kept the child safe, taught self-discipline and public behaviour, attended class and helped with tutoring.[[468]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201190,_92-490)

Formal education was available only to families who could pay for it; lack of state support contributed to low literacy.[[469]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-491) Primary education in reading, writing, and arithmetic might take place at home if parents hired or bought a teacher.[[470]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201187%E2%80%9389-492) Other children attended "public" schools organized by a schoolmaster (_[ludimagister](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludi\_magister "Ludi magister")_) paid by parents.[[471]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011122-493)_Vernae_ (homeborn slave children) might share in-home or public schooling.[[472]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201190-494) Boys and girls received primary education generally from ages 7 to 12, but classes were not segregated by grade or age.[[473]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011107%E2%80%93108,_132-495) Most schools employed [corporal punishment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corporal_punishment "Corporal punishment").[[474]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201193%E2%80%9394-496) For the socially ambitious, education in Greek as well as Latin was necessary.[[475]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201189-497) Schools became more numerous during the Empire, increasing educational opportunities.[[475]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201189-497)

[![Image 69](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/MANNapoli_124545_plato%27s_academy_mosaic_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-MANNapoli_124545_plato%27s_academy_mosaic_%28cropped%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MANNapoli_124545_plato%27s_academy_mosaic_(cropped).jpg)

Mosaic from Pompeii depicting the [Academy of Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academy_of_Plato "Academy of Plato")

At the age of 14, upperclass males made their [rite of passage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexuality_in_ancient_Rome#Rites_of_passage "Sexuality in ancient Rome") into adulthood, and began to learn leadership roles through mentoring from a senior family member or family friend.[[476]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201188,_106-498) Higher education was provided by _[grammatici](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grammarian\_(Greco-Roman) "Grammarian (Greco-Roman)")_ or _[rhetores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhetor "Rhetor")_.[[477]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011109-499) The _grammaticus_ or "grammarian" taught mainly Greek and Latin literature, with history, geography, philosophy or mathematics treated as explications of the text.[[478]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011132-500) With the rise of Augustus, contemporary Latin authors such as Virgil and Livy also became part of the curriculum.[[479]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter2009439,_442-501) The _rhetor_ was a teacher of oratory or public speaking. The art of speaking (_ars dicendi_) was highly prized, and _eloquentia_ ("speaking ability, eloquence") was considered the "glue" of civilized society.[[480]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin2011102%E2%80%93103,_105-502) Rhetoric was not so much a body of knowledge (though it required a command of the [literary canon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literary_canon "Literary canon")[[481]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin2011104%E2%80%93105-503)) as it was a mode of expression that distinguished those who held social power.[[482]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin2011103,_106-504) The ancient model of rhetorical training—"restraint, coolness under pressure, modesty, and good humour"[[483]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin2011110-505)—endured into the 18th century as a Western educational ideal.[[484]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin2011107-506)

In Latin, _illiteratus_ could mean both "unable to read and write" and "lacking in cultural awareness or sophistication".[[485]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris19895-507) Higher education promoted career advancement.[[486]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-508) Urban elites throughout the Empire shared a literary culture imbued with Greek educational ideals (_[paideia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paideia "Paideia")_).[[487]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter2009598-509) Hellenistic cities sponsored schools of higher learning to express cultural achievement.[[488]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaes2011109%E2%80%93110-510) Young Roman men often went abroad to study rhetoric and philosophy, mostly to Athens. The curriculum in the East was more likely to include music and physical training.[[489]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201188-511) On the Hellenistic model, Vespasian [endowed chairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endowed_chair "Endowed chair") of grammar, Latin and Greek rhetoric, and philosophy at Rome, and gave secondary teachers special exemptions from taxes and legal penalties.[[490]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-512) In the Eastern Empire, [Berytus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berytus "Berytus") (present-day [Beirut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beirut "Beirut")) was unusual in offering a Latin education, and became famous for its [school of Roman law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_School_of_Beirut "Law School of Beirut").[[491]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarin201018-513) The cultural movement known as the [Second Sophistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Sophistic "Second Sophistic") (1st–3rd century AD) promoted the assimilation of Greek and Roman social, educational, and esthetic values.[[492]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-514)

Literate women ranged from cultured aristocrats to girls trained to be [calligraphers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calligrapher "Calligrapher") and [scribes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scribe "Scribe").[[493]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-h122-515)[[494]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawson200380-516) The ideal woman in Augustan love poetry was educated and well-versed in the arts.[[495]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-517) Education seems to have been standard for daughters of the senatorial and equestrian orders.[[472]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeachin201190-494) An educated wife was an asset for the socially ambitious household.[[493]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-h122-515)

### Literature

[![Image 70](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ovidiu03.jpg/190px-Ovidiu03.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ovidiu03.jpg)

Statue in [Constanța](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constan%C8%9Ba "Constanța"), Romania (the ancient colony Tomis), commemorating [Ovid's exile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exile_of_Ovid "Exile of Ovid")

[Literature under Augustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustan_literature_(ancient_Rome) "Augustan literature (ancient Rome)"), along with that of the Republic, has been viewed as the "Golden Age" of Latin literature, embodying [classical ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classicism "Classicism").[[496]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts19893-518) The three most influential Classical Latin poets—[Virgil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgil "Virgil"), [Horace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horace "Horace"), and [Ovid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovid "Ovid")—belong to this period. Virgil's _[Aeneid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeneid "Aeneid")_ was a national epic in the manner of the [Homeric epics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homeric_epics "Homeric epics") of Greece. Horace perfected the use of [Greek lyric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_lyric "Greek lyric")[metres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre_(poetry) "Metre (poetry)") in Latin verse. Ovid's erotic poetry was enormously popular, but ran afoul of Augustan morality, contributing to his exile. Ovid's _[Metamorphoses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metamorphoses "Metamorphoses")_ wove together [Greco-Roman mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greco-Roman_mythology "Greco-Roman mythology"); his versions of [Greek myths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology") became a primary source of later [classical mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mythology "Classical mythology"), and his work was hugely influential on [medieval literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medieval_literature "Medieval literature").[[497]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-519) The early [Principate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principate "Principate") produced [satirists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satire "Satire") such as [Persius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persius "Persius") and [Juvenal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juvenal "Juvenal").

The mid-1st through mid-2nd century has conventionally been called the "[Silver Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Latin#Authors_of_the_Silver_Age "Classical Latin")" of Latin literature. The three leading writers—[Seneca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger "Seneca the Younger"), [Lucan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucan "Lucan"), and [Petronius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petronius "Petronius")—committed suicide after incurring [Nero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nero "Nero")'s displeasure. [Epigrammatist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epigram "Epigram") and social observer [Martial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial "Martial") and the epic poet [Statius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statius "Statius"), whose poetry collection _[Silvae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silvae "Silvae")_ influenced [Renaissance literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renaissance_literature "Renaissance literature"),[[498]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-520) wrote during the reign of [Domitian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domitian "Domitian"). Other authors of the Silver Age included [Pliny the Elder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder "Pliny the Elder"), author of the encyclopedic _[Natural History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural\_History\_(Pliny) "Natural History (Pliny)")_; his nephew, [Pliny the Younger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger "Pliny the Younger"); and the historian [Tacitus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tacitus "Tacitus").

The principal Latin prose author of the [Augustan age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustan_literature_(ancient_Rome) "Augustan literature (ancient Rome)") is the [historian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_historiography "Roman historiography")[Livy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livy "Livy"), whose account of [Rome's founding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Founding_of_Rome "Founding of Rome") became the most familiar version in modern-era literature. _[The Twelve Caesars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Twelve\_Caesars "The Twelve Caesars")_ by [Suetonius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suetonius "Suetonius") is a primary source for imperial biography. Among Imperial historians who wrote in Greek are [Dionysius of Halicarnassus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus "Dionysius of Halicarnassus"), [Josephus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josephus "Josephus"), and [Cassius Dio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassius_Dio "Cassius Dio"). Other major Greek authors of the Empire include the biographer [Plutarch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plutarch "Plutarch"), the geographer [Strabo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strabo "Strabo"), and the rhetorician and satirist [Lucian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucian "Lucian").

From the 2nd to the 4th centuries, Christian authors were in active dialogue with the [classical tradition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_tradition "Classical tradition"). [Tertullian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertullian "Tertullian") was one of the earliest prose authors with a distinctly Christian voice. After the [conversion of Constantine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conversion_of_Constantine "Conversion of Constantine"), Latin literature is dominated by the Christian perspective.[[499]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbrecht19971294-521) In the late 4th century, [Jerome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerome "Jerome") produced the Latin translation of the Bible that became authoritative as the [Vulgate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulgate "Vulgate"). Around that same time, [Augustine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo "Augustine of Hippo") wrote _[The City of God against the Pagans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_City\_of\_God\_against\_the\_Pagans "The City of God against the Pagans")_, considered "a masterpiece of Western culture".[[500]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-522)

In contrast to the unity of Classical Latin, the literary esthetic of late antiquity has a [tessellated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tessellation "Tessellation") quality.[[501]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts198970-523) A continuing interest in the religious traditions of Rome prior to Christian dominion is found into the 5th century, with the _Saturnalia_ of [Macrobius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macrobius "Macrobius") and _[The Marriage of Philology and Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Marriage\_of\_Philology\_and\_Mercury "The Marriage of Philology and Mercury")_ of [Martianus Capella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martianus_Capella "Martianus Capella"). Latin poets of late antiquity include [Ausonius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ausonius "Ausonius"), [Prudentius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prudentius "Prudentius"), [Claudian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claudian "Claudian"), and [Sidonius Apollinaris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris "Sidonius Apollinaris").

Religion
--------

The Romans thought of themselves as highly religious, and attributed their success to their collective piety (_[pietas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pietas "Pietas")_) and good relations with the gods (_[pax deorum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pax\_deorum "Pax deorum")_). The archaic religion believed to have come from the earliest [kings of Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kings_of_Rome "Kings of Rome") was the foundation of the _[mos maiorum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mos\_maiorum "Mos maiorum")_, "the way of the ancestors", central to Roman identity.[[502]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-524)

Roman religion was practical and contractual, based on the principle of _[do ut des](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Do\_ut\_des "Do ut des")_, "I give that you might give". Religion depended on knowledge and the [correct practice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthopraxy "Orthopraxy") of prayer, ritual, and sacrifice, not on faith or dogma, although Latin literature preserves learned speculation on the nature of the divine. For ordinary Romans, religion was a part of daily life.[[503]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%BCpke20074-525) Each home had a household shrine to offer prayers and [libations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libation "Libation") to the family's domestic deities. Neighbourhood shrines and sacred places such as springs and groves dotted the city. The [Roman calendar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_calendar "Roman calendar") was structured around religious observances; as many as 135 days were devoted to [religious festivals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_festivals "Roman festivals") and games (_[ludi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludi "Ludi")_).[[504]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-526)

In the wake of the [Republic's collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Roman_Republic "Collapse of the Roman Republic"), state religion adapted to support the new regime. Augustus justified one-man rule with a vast programme of religious revivalism and reform. [Public vows](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vota_pro_salute_rei_publicae "Vota pro salute rei publicae") now were directed at the wellbeing of the emperor. So-called "emperor worship" expanded on a grand scale the traditional [veneration of the ancestral dead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_funerals_and_burial "Roman funerals and burial") and of the _[Genius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genius\_(mythology) "Genius (mythology)")_, the divine [tutelary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tutelary_deity "Tutelary deity") of every individual. Upon death, an emperor could be made a state divinity (_[divus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divus "Divus")_) by vote of the Senate. The [Roman imperial cult](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_imperial_cult "Roman imperial cult"), influenced by [Hellenistic ruler cult](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic_ruler_cult "Hellenistic ruler cult"), became one of the major ways Rome advertised its presence in the provinces and cultivated shared cultural identity. Cultural precedent in the Eastern provinces facilitated a rapid dissemination of Imperial cult, extending as far as [Najran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Najran "Najran"), in present-day [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia").[[w]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-528) Rejection of the state religion became tantamount to treason.

The Romans are known for the [great number of deities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Roman_deities "List of Roman deities") they honoured. As the Romans extended their territories, their general policy was to promote stability among diverse peoples by absorbing local deities and cults rather than eradicating them,[[x]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-529) building temples that framed local theology within Roman religion. Inscriptions throughout the Empire record the side-by-side worship of local and Roman deities, including dedications made by Romans to local gods.[[506]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-530) By the height of the Empire, numerous [syncretic or reinterpreted gods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interpretatio_romana "Interpretatio romana") were cultivated, among them cults of [Cybele](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cybele "Cybele"), [Isis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isis "Isis"), [Epona](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epona "Epona"), and of solar gods such as [Mithras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mithras "Mithras") and [Sol Invictus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sol_Invictus "Sol Invictus"), found as far north as [Roman Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Britain "Roman Britain"). Because Romans had never been obligated to cultivate one god or cult only, [religious tolerance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_tolerance "Religious tolerance") was not an issue.[[507]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-531)

[Mystery religions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mystery_religions "Mystery religions"), which offered initiates salvation in the afterlife, were a matter of personal choice, practiced in addition to one's [family rites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sacra_gentilicia "Sacra gentilicia") and public religion. The mysteries, however, involved exclusive oaths and secrecy, which conservative Romans viewed with suspicion as characteristic of "[magic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magic_in_the_Greco-Roman_world "Magic in the Greco-Roman world")", conspiracy (_coniuratio_), and subversive activity. Thus, sporadic and sometimes brutal attempts were made to suppress religionists. In Gaul, the power of the [druids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druid "Druid") was checked, first by forbidding Roman citizens to belong to the order, and then by banning druidism altogether. However, Celtic traditions were reinterpreted within the context of Imperial theology, and a new [Gallo-Roman religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallo-Roman_religion "Gallo-Roman religion") coalesced; its capital at the [Sanctuary of the Three Gauls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanctuary_of_the_Three_Gauls "Sanctuary of the Three Gauls") established precedent for Western cult as a form of Roman-provincial identity.[[508]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-532) The monotheistic rigour of [Judaism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism "Judaism") posed difficulties for Roman policy that led at times to compromise and granting of special exemptions. Tertullian noted that Judaism, unlike Christianity, was considered a _[religio licita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religio\_licita "Religio licita")_, "legitimate religion". The [Jewish–Roman wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_wars "Jewish–Roman wars") resulted from political as well as religious conflicts; the [siege of Jerusalem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70) "Siege of Jerusalem (70)") in 70 AD led to the sacking of the [Second Temple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Temple "Second Temple") and the dispersal of Jewish political power (see [Jewish diaspora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_diaspora "Jewish diaspora")).

[![Image 71](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Stele_Licinia_Amias_Terme_67646.jpg/250px-Stele_Licinia_Amias_Terme_67646.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stele_Licinia_Amias_Terme_67646.jpg)

A 3rd-century funerary stele is among the [earliest Christian inscriptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Christian_inscriptions "Early Christian inscriptions"), written in both Greek and Latin.

Christianity emerged in [Roman Judaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaea_(Roman_province) "Judaea (Roman province)") as a [Jewish religious sect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_Christian "Jewish Christian") in the 1st century and gradually [spread](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spread_of_Christianity "Spread of Christianity") out of [Jerusalem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity "Jerusalem in Christianity") throughout the Empire and beyond. Imperially authorized persecutions were limited and sporadic, with martyrdoms occurring most often under the authority of local officials.[[509]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-533)[Tacitus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tacitus "Tacitus") reports that after the [Great Fire of Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome "Great Fire of Rome") in AD 64, the emperor attempted to deflect blame from himself onto the Christians.[[510]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-annals-xv-44-534) A major persecution occurred under the emperor [Domitian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domitian "Domitian")[[511]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-535) and a [persecution in 177](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persecution_in_Lyon "Persecution in Lyon") took place at Lugdunum, the Gallo-Roman religious capital. A letter from [Pliny the Younger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger "Pliny the Younger"), governor of [Bithynia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bithynia "Bithynia"), describes his persecution and executions of Christians.[[512]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-536) The [Decian persecution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decian_persecution "Decian persecution") of 246–251 seriously threatened the [Christian Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Church "Christian Church"), but ultimately strengthened Christian defiance.[[513]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-537)[Diocletian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diocletian "Diocletian") undertook the [most severe persecution of Christians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diocletianic_Persecution "Diocletianic Persecution"), from 303 to 311.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-:0-18)

From the 2nd century onward, the [Church Fathers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_Fathers "Church Fathers") condemned the diverse religions practiced throughout the Empire as "pagan".[[514]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowersockBrownGrabar1999625-538) In the early 4th century, [Constantine I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantine_I "Constantine I") became the first emperor to [convert to Christianity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convert_to_Christianity "Convert to Christianity"). He supported the Church financially and made laws that favored it, but the new religion was already successful, having moved from less than 50,000 to over a million adherents between 150 and 250.[[515]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-539) Constantine and his successors banned public sacrifice while tolerating other traditional practices. Constantine never engaged in a [purge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Purge "Purge"),[[516]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Leithart-540) there were no "pagan martyrs" during his reign,[[517]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-541) and people who had not converted to Christianity remained in important positions at court.[[516]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Leithart-540): 302 [Julian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate "Julian the Apostate") attempted to revive traditional public sacrifice and [Hellenistic religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic_religion "Hellenistic religion"), but met Christian resistance and lack of popular support.[[518]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-542)

[![Image 72](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Pantheon_Rom_1_cropped.jpg/250px-Pantheon_Rom_1_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pantheon_Rom_1_cropped.jpg)

The [Pantheon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome "Pantheon, Rome") in Rome, a [Roman temple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_temple "Roman temple") originally built under [Augustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustus "Augustus"), later converted into a [Catholic church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_architecture "Church architecture") in the 7th century[[519]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-543)

Christians of the 4th century believed the conversion of Constantine showed that Christianity had triumphed over paganism (in Heaven) and little further action besides such rhetoric was necessary.[[520]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-544) Thus, their focus shifted towards [heresy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity "Heresy in Christianity").[[521]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-545)[[522]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Brown_1998-546) According to [Peter Brown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Brown_(historian) "Peter Brown (historian)"), "In most areas, polytheists were not molested, and apart from a few ugly incidents of local violence, Jewish communities also enjoyed a century of stable, even privileged, existence".[[522]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Brown_1998-546): 641–643 [[523]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-547) There were anti-pagan laws, but they were not generally enforced; through the 6th century, centers of paganism existed in Athens, Gaza, Alexandria, and elsewhere.[[524]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-548)

According to recent Jewish scholarship, toleration of the Jews was maintained under Christian emperors.[[525]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewer2005127-549) This did not extend to [heretics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_heresy "Christian heresy"):[[525]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewer2005127-549)[Theodosius I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodosius_I "Theodosius I") made multiple laws and acted against alternate forms of Christianity,[[526]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-550) and heretics were persecuted and killed by both the government and the church throughout Late Antiquity. Non-Christians were not persecuted until the 6th century. Rome's original religious hierarchy and ritual influenced Christian forms,[[527]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%BCpke2007406%E2%80%93426-551)[[528]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-552) and many pre-Christian practices survived in Christian festivals and local traditions.

Legacy
------

[![Image 73](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/2015_Virginia_State_House_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia_01.JPG/250px-2015_Virginia_State_House_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia_01.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2015_Virginia_State_House_-_Richmond,_Virginia_01.JPG)

[![Image 74](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Maison_carr%C3%A9e_%283%29.jpg/250px-Maison_carr%C3%A9e_%283%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Maison_carr%C3%A9e_(3).jpg)

Several states claimed to be the [Roman Empire's successor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Succession_of_the_Roman_Empire "Succession of the Roman Empire"). The [Holy Roman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire "Holy Roman Empire") was established in 800 when [Pope Leo III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pope_Leo_III "Pope Leo III") crowned [Charlemagne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charlemagne "Charlemagne") as [Roman emperor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_emperor "Roman emperor"). The [Russian Tsardom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia "Tsardom of Russia"), as inheritor of the Byzantine Empire's [Orthodox Christian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church "Eastern Orthodox Church") tradition, counted itself the [Third Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_Rome "Third Rome") (Constantinople having been the second), in accordance with the concept of [translatio imperii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translatio_imperii "Translatio imperii").[[529]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-553) The last Eastern Roman titular, [Andreas Palaiologos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andreas_Palaiologos "Andreas Palaiologos"), sold the title of Emperor of Constantinople to [Charles VIII of France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France "Charles VIII of France"); upon Charles' death, Palaiologos reclaimed the title and on his death granted it to [Ferdinand and Isabella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_and_Isabella "Ferdinand and Isabella") and their successors, who never used it. When the [Ottomans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire"), who based their state on the Byzantine model, took Constantinople in 1453, [Mehmed II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mehmed_II "Mehmed II") established his capital there and claimed to sit on the throne of the Roman Empire.[[530]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-554) He even launched an [invasion of Otranto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_invasion_of_Otranto "Ottoman invasion of Otranto") with the purpose of re-uniting the Empire, which was aborted by his death. In the medieval West, "Roman" came to mean the church and the Catholic Pope. The Greek form [Romaioi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romaioi "Romaioi") remained attached to the Greek-speaking Christian population of the Byzantine Empire and is still used by [Greeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeks "Greeks").[[531]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-555)

The Roman Empire's control of the Italian Peninsula influenced [Italian nationalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_nationalism "Italian nationalism") and the [unification of Italy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unification_of_Italy "Unification of Italy") (_[Risorgimento](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Risorgimento "Risorgimento")_) in 1861.[[532]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-556)

In the United States, the [founders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States "Founding Fathers of the United States") were educated in the [classical tradition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_tradition "Classical tradition"),[[533]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-557) and used classical models for [landmarks in Washington, D.C.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_National_Historic_Landmarks_in_Washington,_D.C.
