"List of National Historic Landmarks in Washington, D.C.")[[534]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Meinig-558)[[535]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-vale-559)[[536]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-korn-560)[[537]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-561) The founders saw [Athenian democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Athenian_democracy "Athenian democracy") and [Roman republicanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_republic "Roman republic") as models for the [mixed constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mixed_constitution "Mixed constitution"), but regarded the emperor as a figure of tyranny.[[538]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-562)

Notes
-----

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-3)**Modern scholars often date the end of the "classical" or "unified" Roman Empire in AD 395.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-1) This is a modern convention, as the Empire continued to be seen as a single state even after the supposed "split" of 395, which was in fact one of many splits since 286.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-2)
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-5)**Fig. 1. Regions east of the [Euphrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euphrates "Euphrates") were held only in the years 116–117.
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-16)**In 286, Emperor Diocletian divided the Roman Empire into two administrative units–[East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire "Eastern Roman Empire") and [West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire "Western Roman Empire")–an arrangement that periodically returned until the two halves were permanently divided in 395.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-:1-6) Although the halves were independent in practice, the Romans continued to consider the Roman Empire to be a single undivided state with two co-equal emperors until the fall of the western half in 476/480.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-:1-6) Although emperors at times governed from other cities (notably [Mediolanum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediolanum "Mediolanum") and [Ravenna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ravenna "Ravenna") in the West and [Nicomedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicomedia "Nicomedia") in the East), Rome remained the _[de jure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De\_jure "De jure")_ capital of the entire Roman Empire. In 330, Emperor [Constantine I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantine_the_Great "Constantine the Great") made Constantinople a second and new capital of the empire ("Second Rome" or "New Rome").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-7)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-8)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-9)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-10)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-11)[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-12) For a time, mostly over the course of the later decades of the fourth century, Rome continued to hold greater symbolic status on account of its greater antiquity as imperial capital.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-13) From at least 361 onwards, senators belonging to the [new senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_senate "Byzantine senate") in Constantinople enjoyed the same status and privileges as senators of the [Roman Senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Senate "Roman Senate"), to which the new senate was largely identical.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-14) By 450, Constantinople was much grander in size and adornment than Rome and unquestionably senior in status.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-15)
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-19)**In 1204, the crusaders of the [Fourth Crusade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourth_Crusade "Fourth Crusade") captured Constantinople and established the [Latin Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_Empire "Latin Empire"). The city remained under foreign rule until 1261, when it was captured by the [Empire of Nicaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea "Empire of Nicaea") (a Byzantine/Roman successor state). Nicaea is usually considered the "legitimate" continuation of the Roman Empire during the "interregnum" 1204–1261 (over its rivals in [Trebizond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond "Empire of Trebizond") and [Thessalonica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_of_Thessalonica "Empire of Thessalonica")) since it managed to retake Constantinople.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTETreadgold1997734-17) Whether there was an interregnum at all is debatable given that the crusaders envisioned the Latin Empire to be the same empire as its predecessor (and not a new state).[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-:0-18)
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-25)**Abbreviated "HS". Prices and values are usually expressed in sesterces.
6.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-27)**The Ottomans sometimes called their state the "Empire of [Rûm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%BBm "Rûm")" ([Ottoman Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language "Ottoman Turkish language"): دولت علنإه روم, [lit.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literal_translation "Literal translation")'Exalted State of Rome'). In this sense, it could be argued that a "Roman" Empire survived until the early 20th century.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-26)
7.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-36)**[Augustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustus "Augustus") avoided any association with the ancient [kings of Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_of_Rome "King of Rome"). Augustus had replaced his first name with _Imperator_, a title regularly used by [Julius Caesar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julius_Caesar "Julius Caesar"), thus becoming _Imperator Caesar Augustus_, which further linked the title with his position. _Imperator_ did not acquire the meaning of "ruler" until the late 1st century.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-SimonHornblower-35) Both _[Caesar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesar\_(title) "Caesar (title)")_ and _[Augustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustus\_(title) "Augustus (title)")_ evolved into formal titles, the former denoting the heir and the latter the monarch. In some languages, _Caesar_ became the origin of the word "[emperor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emperor "Emperor")", such as in German (_[Kaiser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaiser "Kaiser")_) and some Slavic languages (_[Tsar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsar "Tsar")_).
8.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-61)**[Prudentius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prudentius "Prudentius") (348–413) in particular Christianizes the theme in his poetry.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-60)[St. Augustine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Augustine "St. Augustine"), however, distinguished between the secular and eternal "Rome" in _[The City of God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De\_Civitate\_Dei "De Civitate Dei")._ See also [Fears, J. Rufus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._Rufus_Fears "J. Rufus Fears") (1981), "The Cult of Jupiter and Roman Imperial Ideology", _Aufstieg und Niedergang der römischen Welt_, vol.II, p.136, on how Classical Roman ideology influenced Christian Imperial doctrine, Bang, Peter Fibiger (2011), "The King of Kings: Universal Hegemony, Imperial Power, and a New Comparative History of Rome", _The Roman Empire in Context: Historical and Comparative Perspectives_, John Wiley & Sons and the Greek concept of globalism (_[oikouménē](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecumene "Ecumene")_).
9.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-diglossia_78-0)**It has been called a state of bilingualism but that's only true of the educated and so Bruno Rochette suggests it's more appropriate as a [diglossia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diglossia "Diglossia") but concedes this still does not adequately explain it, as Greek was "high" against Latins "Super-high".[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERochette2018123-74) Latin experienced a period of spreading from the second century BCE, and especially in the western provinces, but not as much in the eastern provinces.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-75) In the east, Greek was always the dominant language, a left over influence from the [Hellenistic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic_period "Hellenistic period") that predates the Empire.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTERochette2018108-76)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-77)
10.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-127)**The _civis_ ("citizen") stands in explicit contrast to a _[peregrina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peregrinus\_(Roman) "Peregrinus (Roman)")_, a foreign or non-Roman woman[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-125) In the form of legal marriage called _conubium,_ the father's legal status determined the child's, but _conubium_ required that both spouses be free citizens. A soldier, for instance, was banned from marrying while in service, but if he formed a long-term union with a local woman while stationed in the provinces, he could marry her legally after he was discharged, and any children they had would be considered the offspring of citizens—in effect granting the woman retroactive citizenship. The ban was in place from the time of Augustus until it was rescinded by [Septimius Severus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Septimius_Severus "Septimius Severus") in 197 AD.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-126)
11.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-142)**The others are [ancient Athens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Greece "Slavery in ancient Greece"), and in the modern era [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil "Slavery in Brazil"), the [Caribbean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_the_British_and_French_Caribbean "Slavery in the British and French Caribbean"), and the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States "Slavery in the United States")
12.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-172)**That senator was Tiberius Claudius Gordianus[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoardman2000219-171)
13.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-175)**The relation of the equestrian order to the "public horse" and Roman cavalry parades and demonstrations (such as the _[Lusus Troiae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lusus\_Troiae "Lusus Troiae")_) is complex, but those who participated in the latter seem, for instance, to have been the _equites_ who were accorded the high-status (and quite limited) seating at the theatre by the _[Lex Roscia theatralis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lex\_Roscia\_theatralis "Lex Roscia theatralis")_. Senators could not possess the "public horse".[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiseman197078%E2%80%9379-174)
14.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-178)**Ancient Gades, in Roman Spain (now [Cádiz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%A1diz "Cádiz")), and Patavium, in the Celtic north of Italy (now [Padua](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Padua "Padua")), were atypically wealthy cities, and having 500 equestrians in one city was unusual.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-177)
15.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-234)**This practice was established in the Republic; see for instance the case of [Contrebian water rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaius_Valerius_Flaccus#Contrebian_water_rights "Gaius Valerius Flaccus") heard by G. Valerius Flaccus as governor of [Hispania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hispania "Hispania") in the 90s–80s BC.
16.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-245)**This was the _vicesima libertatis_, "the twentieth for freedom"[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter2009187-237)
17.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-295)**The college of _centonarii_ is an elusive topic in scholarship, since they are also widely attested as urban firefighters.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVout2009212-293)[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Liu-294) Historian [Jinyu Liu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinyu_Liu "Jinyu Liu") sees them as "primarily tradesmen and/or manufacturers engaged in the production and distribution of low- or medium-quality woolen textiles and clothing, including felt and its products".[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-Liu-294)
18.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-312)**Julius Caesar first applied the Latin word _oppidum_ to this type of settlement, and even called [Avaricum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avaricum "Avaricum") ([Bourges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourges "Bourges"), France), a center of the [Bituriges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bituriges_Cubi "Bituriges Cubi"), an _urbs_, "city". Archaeology indicates that _oppida_ were centers of religion, trade (including import/export), and industrial production, walled for the purposes of defence, but they may not have been inhabited by concentrated populations year-round.[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-311)
19.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-358)**Such as the [Consualia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consualia "Consualia") and the [October Horse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October_Horse "October Horse") sacrifice.[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-357)
20.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-396)**Scholars are divided in their relative emphasis on the athletic and dance elements of these exercises: Lee, H. (1984). "Athletics and the Bikini Girls from Piazza Armerina". _Stadion_. **10**: 45–75. sees them as gymnasts, while Torelli thinks they are dancers at the games.[[376]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-395)
21.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-457)**[Clifford Ando](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clifford_Ando "Clifford Ando") posed the question as "what good would 'posted edicts' do in a world of low literacy?'.[[436]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndo2000101see_also_p._87_on_%22the_government's_obsessive_documentation%22-456)
22.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-464)**Political slogans and obscenities are widely preserved as graffiti in Pompeii: Antonio Varone, _Erotica Pompeiana: Love Inscriptions on the Walls of Pompeii_ ("L'Erma" di Bretschneider, 2002). Soldiers sometimes inscribed [sling bullets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sling_bullet "Sling bullet") with aggressive messages: Phang, "Military Documents, Languages, and Literacy," p. 300.
23.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-528)**The _[caesareum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesareum "Caesareum")_ at Najaran was possibly known later as the "Kaaba of Najran"[[505]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_note-527)
24.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-529)**"This mentality," notes John T. Koch, "lay at the core of the genius of cultural assimilation which made the Roman Empire possible"; entry on "Interpretatio romana," in _Celtic Culture: A Historical Encyclopedia_ (ABC-Clio, 2006), p. 974.

96.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-Helix_105-0)**Hélix, Laurence (2011). _Histoire de la langue française_. Ellipses Edition Marketing S.A. p.7. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)")[978-2-7298-6470-5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-2-7298-6470-5 "Special:BookSources/978-2-7298-6470-5"). Le déclin du Gaulois et sa disparition ne s'expliquent pas seulement par des pratiques culturelles spécifiques: Lorsque les Romains conduits par César envahirent la Gaule, au 1er siecle avant J.-C., celle-ci romanisa de manière progressive et profonde. Pendant près de 500 ans, la fameuse période gallo-romaine, le gaulois et le latin parlé coexistèrent; au VIe siècle encore; le temoignage de Grégoire de Tours atteste la survivance de la langue gauloise.

212.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter2009177%E2%80%93179Most_government_records_that_are_preserved_come_from_Roman_Egypt,_where_the_climate_preserved_the_papyri._226-0)**[Potter (2009)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#CITEREFPotter2009), pp.177–179, Most government records that are preserved come from Roman Egypt, where the climate preserved the papyri..
213.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter2009179The_exclusion_of_Egypt_from_the_senatorial_provinces_dates_to_the_rise_of_Octavian_before_he_became_Augustus:_Egypt_had_been_the_stronghold_of_his_last_opposition,_[[Mark_Antony]]_and_his_ally_[[Cleopatra]]._227-0)**[Potter (2009)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire#CITEREFPotter2009), p.179, The exclusion of Egypt from the senatorial provinces dates to the rise of Octavian before he became Augustus: Egypt had been the stronghold of his last opposition, [Mark Antony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Antony "Mark Antony") and his ally [Cleopatra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cleopatra "Cleopatra")..
